        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202405921')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="bd072b729def4166afcf4945251b301b"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="bd072b729def4166afcf4945251b301b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b2b7647e-6f3e-4b8e-9155-60649162b2fc'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = '0DFBF01AE767D4E7E0B333607E9D50AA';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d38791525" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) stands out as the most prospective anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with significant potential for commercial applications. However, some long-standing and intractable obstacles, like low first coulombic efficiency (ICE), poor rate capability, storage capacity, and cycling stability, have severely hindered the conversion process from laboratory to commercialization. The above-mentioned issues are closely related to Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics, surface chemistry, and internal pseudo-graphitic carbon content. Herein, constructing molybdenum-modified hard carbon solid spheres (Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0), both the ion transfer kinetics, surface chemistry, and internal pseudo-graphitic carbon content are comprehensively improved. Specifically, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 with higher pseudo-graphitic carbon content provides a large number of active sites and a more stable layer structure, resulting in improved sodium storage capacity, rate performance, and cycling stability. Moreover, the lower defect density and specific surface area of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 further enhance ICE and sodium storage capacity. Consequently, the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 anode achieves a high capacity of 410.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and an ICE of 83.9% at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the material exhibits exceptional rate capability and cycling stability, maintaining a capacity of 202.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 214.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202405921-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202405921-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Energy serves as the cornerstone for both human development and survival. The most prominent lithium-ion batteries are facing challenges in supporting the development of large-scale stationary energy storage systems, due to the cost and resource constraints.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0001_R_d38791517e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various candidates, sustainable SIBs are regarded as the primary choice for large-scale energy storage with the superiority of ample reserves and cost-effectiveness of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0002_R_d38791517e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">2</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0003_R_d38791517e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The anode material is the core of SIBs, determining critical performance parameters such as specific capacity, rate capability, cycle performance, energy density, and so on. The structural characteristics and electrochemical properties of anode materials take a pivotal part in the overall sodium storage performance of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0004_R_d38791517e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0005_R_d38791517e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the superiorities of abundant resources, straightforward preparation, considerable sodium storage capabilities, low average operating potential, and chemical stability, hard carbon has been regarded as the most promising anode in SIBs for commercial application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0006_R_d38791517e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Molecules">6</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0007_R_d38791517e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Asian J.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The internal structure of HC consists of isotropic graphite-like microcrystals with low crystallinity and disordered arrangements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0008_R_d38791517e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, these graphite microcrystals have large layer spacing, numerous defects, and large specific surface area, which significantly consume excessive electrolyte and generate additional irreversible capacity by generating unstable solid electrolyte interfaces in the initial discharge process. The above structural defects and poor Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics result in low ICE, poor rate capability, and cycling stability, which severely hinder the conversion process of HC anodes from laboratory to commercialization. Developing high-performance HC with high ICE, superior rate capability and cycling stability is an urgent goal for large-scale stationary energy storage systems.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, many strategies, like electrolyte modification,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0009_R_d38791517e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0010_R_d38791517e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surface functionalization<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0011_R_d38791517e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0012_R_d38791517e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (heteroatom doping and defect shielding), and structural designing<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0013_R_d38791517e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#smll202405921-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0015_R_d38791517e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (modulation of layer spacing, multidimensional scaling morphology, and hard-soft-carbon composites) have been proposed to improve the electrochemical performance of HC. Among them, surface functionalization and structural design are the two most promising strategies. Yu et&nbsp;al. prepare nitrogen-doped porous carbon fibers, which deliver a high capacity of 296&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a relatively low ICE of 46% due to the irreversible adsorption and the formation of SEI film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0016_R_d38791517e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nanoscale">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, F,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0017_R_d38791517e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon Energy">17</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0018_R_d38791517e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Trans. Tianjin Univ.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0019_R_d38791517e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0020_R_d38791517e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> B,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0021_R_d38791517e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. have also been introduced to modify HC and achieved enhanced electrochemical performance. Pan et&nbsp;al. propose vanadium-modified hard carbon material with an optimized structure and reduced layer spacing, showing a high capacity of 379&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Nevertheless, its increased degree of defects leads to low ICE (68.7%) and poor performance at high current densities (90&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0022_R_d38791517e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Carbon Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Regrettably, all the above strategies fail to simultaneously and comprehensively improve the overall electrochemical performance (ICE, rate, capacity, cycling stability), which is closely related to the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics, surface, and internal structure of HC. Therefore, it is essential to propose a facile and comprehensive strategy to simultaneously modulate kinetics, surface, and internal structure for high-performance HC anodes.</p>
         
         <p>Universally, the charge–discharge process of HC displays two distinguished potential zones bounded by 0.1 V: a sloping region and a plateau region. Xu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0023_R_d38791517e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> put forth an extended theory of “adsorption-insertion,” which posits that highly disordered carbons (possessed by unmodified HC) with layer spacing larger than 0.40&nbsp;nm allow for unrestricted movement of Na<sup>+</sup> ions by a process similar to adsorption, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0001">1</a> (the left region). It brings about an increase in sloping capacity (&gt;0.1&nbsp;V) and generates special types of “defects” on the pore surface and edges. Meanwhile, too many defects lead to more irreversible capacity, resulting in low ICE and poor cycling stability, which is not favorable for sodium storage. The platform behavior (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V) of somewhat ordered pseudo-graphitic carbons with layer spacing ranging from 0.36 to 0.40&nbsp;nm is attributed to “interlayer insertion”, in common with the insertion of lithium-ions within graphite. The theoretical capacity estimate for pseudo-graphitic carbon interlayer insertion is 279 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, correlating to the formation of NaC<sub>8</sub>. Graphitic-like carbons with layer spacing less than 0.36&nbsp;nm contribute less to sodium storage capacity due to their smaller interlayer spacing. This also means that the structure of HC-based materials can be improved by constructing suitable pseudo-graphite layer spacings, promoting the interlayer insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in order to enhance sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0023_R_d38791517e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0024_R_d38791517e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Environ. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, adjusting a suitable heteroatom doping level for the specific surface area and surface defects of HC-based anodes can suppress the irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte, thereby improving the reversible capacity and ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0025_R_d38791517e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a>-<a href="#smll202405921-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0027_R_d38791517e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J Energy Chem">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both are combined to create a full range of high-performance HC anode materials with high capacity, high ICE, and excellent cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202405921-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9b1a50c3-f5ea-4065-98eb-4af8bb142bd6/smll202405921-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9b1a50c3-f5ea-4065-98eb-4af8bb142bd6/smll202405921-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/438e56be-e547-4dcf-832f-66f123fa5e20/smll202405921-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9b1a50c3-f5ea-4065-98eb-4af8bb142bd6/smll202405921-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202405921-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202405921" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the synthesis process, the composition of graphite domains of hard carbons and the sodium storage mode and contribution. Synthesis process diagram and structural illustrations of molybdenum-modified hard carbon (Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC) materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In this work, we report a high-pseudo-graphitic carbon hard carbon strategy by constructing Mo-modified hard carbon solid spheres, which comprehensively improve the sodium storage capacity, cycling stability, ICE, and rate performance of HC. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0001">1</a> (the right region), the molybdenum carbide-modified hard carbon solid spheres (Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0) possess an ample amount of pseudo-graphite carbon serving as sites for the insertion/extraction of sodium ions, significantly enhancing the low plateau capacity of sodium-ion batteries. What's more, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibits lower defect density and reduced specific surface area, which reduces the occurrence of irreversible reactions and improves ICE and cycling stability. Meanwhile, the Na<sup>+</sup> transport behavior, interfacial impedance, and electrode kinetics are significantly enhanced, which is conducive to the improvement of rate performance. Consequently, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 demonstrates a comprehensive enhanced performance with a much higher ICE of 83.9% (vs. 72.6%) and a high capacity of 410.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, the cycling stability (214.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 800 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and rate performance (202.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>) of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 are also greatly enhanced.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202405921-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202405921-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202405921-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202405921-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The microstructure and morphology of HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 are observed using field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2a,b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Scanning electron microscope (SEM) morphological images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information) indicate the presence of obvious aggregation and an uneven distribution of carbon spheres in HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0028_R_d38791517e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2a,b</a>, the hard carbon microspheres of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 are homogeneous, with an average diameter of 500–600&nbsp;nm and no aggregation occurs. This may be attributed to the effectiveness of MoO<sub>3</sub> in nucleating carbon spheres and regulating their uniformity. Other SEM images of samples with different amounts of MoO<sub>3</sub> support this observation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b–d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202405921-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8ca549f3-1b9b-4e24-ab76-cff2f6eff8c2/smll202405921-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8ca549f3-1b9b-4e24-ab76-cff2f6eff8c2/smll202405921-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4ed8c0fc-9c56-4ae9-9963-47a1cd2bbb16/smll202405921-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8ca549f3-1b9b-4e24-ab76-cff2f6eff8c2/smll202405921-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202405921-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202405921" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural, morphological characterization and elemental composition analysis of molybdenum-modified hard carbon materials (shorted as Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0). a,b) SEM images, c,d) TEM images, e,f) HRTEM images and g–k) EDX images of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, where the blue and green circles highlight regions correspond to pseudo-graphitic carbons and highly disordered carbons, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Transmission electron microscope (TEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2c</a>) reveal that Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 hard carbon microspheres have a completely solid internal structure, which is advantageous for improving electrode energy density, reducing stress during cycling, and enhancing electrode material stability. High-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit that both HC and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 materials are disordered carbons, with the variation being the presence of Mo<sub>2</sub>C nanoparticles on the surface of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 carbon spheres. The (221) plane of Mo<sub>2</sub>C crystal can be identified by the lattice stripes with a spacing of 0.178&nbsp;nm. Additionally, the presence of lattice defects on the phase boundaries offers more active sites, which significantly improves the electrochemical performance of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0029_R_d38791517e714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As observed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2f</a>, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 material has more short-range ordered pseudo-graphitic carbons (blue circles), while HC is primarily composed of randomly oriented highly disordered carbons (yellow-green circles). Pseudo-graphitic carbons have d-spacing ranging from 0.36 to 0.40&nbsp;nm, showing the plateau behavior attributed to “interlayer insertion,” which is akin to the insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> ions in graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0030_R_d38791517e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the low-voltage platform capacity of SIBs is significantly enhanced.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0031_R_d38791517e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mappings of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2g–k</a>) show that C and O elements are uniformly dispersed on the hard carbon microspheres, while Mo elements are enriched along the Mo<sub>2</sub>C crystal, with a small amount uniformly dispersed on the microspheres. EDX analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) confirms the presence of C, O, and Mo elements on Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 hard carbon microspheres (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), with Mo content being relatively low (1.4 wt.%).</p>
            
            <p>As X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns observed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3a</a>, all samples exhibit broad peaks centered at 2θ ≈ 22° and 2θ ≈ 43°, in correspondence to the (002) crystal plane of stacked graphene sheets and the (100) crystal plane of sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized hexagonal carbon, respectively. The above results indicate that the prepared carbon materials possess a highly amorphous nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0032_R_d38791517e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other diffraction peaks of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 are well-indexed to orthorhombic Mo<sub>2</sub>C (JCPDS:31-0871).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0033_R_d38791517e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ChemSusChem">33</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0034_R_d38791517e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the (002) diffraction peaks are asymmetric, which is attributed to the mixed polycrystalline structure of pseudo-graphitic carbons, graphitic-like carbons, and highly disordered carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0035_R_d38791517e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After Mo<sub>2</sub>C modification, the (002) peak position of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 shifts to a higher diffraction angle. This change indicates that the reduction in interlayer spacing after Mo<sub>2</sub>C modification promotes the conversion of highly disordered carbons to pseudo-graphitic carbons and forms a more ordered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0002">2</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0036_R_d38791517e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Fuel Process. Technol.">36</a>-<a href="#smll202405921-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0038_R_d38791517e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202405921-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bd980f38-6186-4df8-b87f-a7442fb719be/smll202405921-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bd980f38-6186-4df8-b87f-a7442fb719be/smll202405921-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a6d2b64e-55f1-4e93-8d9c-4b68cfe99284/smll202405921-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bd980f38-6186-4df8-b87f-a7442fb719be/smll202405921-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202405921-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202405921" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural, chemical composition analysis, physical parameter fitting and comparison of molybdenum-modified hard carbon materials. a) XRD patterns, b) Raman spectra, c,d) Comparison of physical parameters, e) Nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherm, f) SAXS patterns of samples, g) XPS spectra, High-resolution XPS fitting spectra of h) C 1s and i) Mo 3d of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">This may be attributed to the insertion of partial molybdenum atoms into interlayers of graphite, forming a composite structure of graphite and molybdenum carbide and reducing the interlayer spacing. Furthermore, molybdenum carbide may affect the van der Waals forces between layers, resulting in structural distortion and altering interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0039_R_d38791517e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To determine the transition process of highly disordered carbons, pseudo-graphitic carbons, and graphitic-like carbons with the Mo content in the precursor, a profile fitting process for the (002) peak is simulated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0022_R_d38791517e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Carbon Energy">22</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0023_R_d38791517e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Each individual peak represents a specific carbon phase, which can be allowed for the calculation of the physical parameters depicted in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202405921-tbl-0001">1</a>. As the molybdenum content in the precursor is augmented, a fraction of the highly disordered carbons with interlayer spacing larger than 0.40&nbsp;nm gradually undergo a transition into pseudo-graphitic carbons, which account for 28.88%, 40.43%, and 69.53% in HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, respectively. When the Mo content in the precursor increases to 7.5&nbsp;mmol, the highly disordered phase completely transforms into pseudo-graphitic phase and graphitic-like phase appearances. Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 has the largest amount of pseudo-graphitic carbons (69.53%), which provide large numbers of active sites for sodium ion insertion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3c</a>). With further increases in the Mo content in the precursor, pseudo-graphitic carbons gradually transform into graphitic-like carbons, which leads to a severe contraction of d-spacing and increasing the difficulty of sodium ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0040_R_d38791517e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10, pseudo-graphitic carbons decrease to 41.88%, and graphitic-like carbons dominate. Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202405921-tbl-0001">1</a> also summarizes the average width of the ordered nanodomains between graphene sheets (L<sub>a</sub>), the thickness of coherent domains (L<sub>c</sub>), and the number of graphene sheets (N) evaluated by Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202405921-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202405921-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0037_R_d38791517e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Carbon">37</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0041_R_d38791517e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0042_R_d38791517e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Small">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202405921-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/74c9697d-589f-4ef8-a74c-5684180fcb7b/smll202405921-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202405921:smll202405921-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202405921-math-0001.png">

Compared to HC, as the Mo content in the precursor increases, L<sub>a</sub>, L<sub>c</sub>, and N gradually increase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3d</a>). This can be attributed to the fact that the introduction of Mo in the precursor promotes the conversion of highly disordered carbon into pseudo-graphitic and graphitic-like carbon, increasing the number of ordered layer nanodomains, that is, enhancing the degree of graphitization. The detailed XRD spectra analysis clearly illustrates the transformation of the carbon microcrystal phase during the addition of Mo to the precursor, which involves a transition from the highly disordered phase to pseudo-graphite phase and finally to graphite-like phase. The transition occurs gradually, with the simultaneous presence of various carbon phases, resulting in a sophisticated mechanism for sodium storage.</div>

            <p>The fitted Raman spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3b</a> also confirm the structural evolution of carbon microcrystals. The Raman spectra of all anodes display two distinctive peaks at ≈1335 (D band) and 1585 cm<sup>−1</sup> (G band), which are associated with the disordered and graphitic carbon structures and their respective vibrational modes. Therefore, the extent of disorganization in the structure can be deeply investigated through the A<sub>D</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> area ratio. The Raman spectra have been effectively reconstructed to acquire a more complete insight into the structural evolution of samples. The five discrete peaks center at 1180, 1360, 1500, 1585, and 1620 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are associated with the D<sub>4</sub>, D<sub>1</sub>, D<sub>3</sub>, G, and D<sub>2</sub> bands, respectively. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The ideal vibrations between carbon atoms in a symmetrical E<sub>2g</sub> graphite lattice are represented by the G band, whereas the vibrations between carbon atoms within disordered graphite lattices with A<sub>1g</sub> and E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry are represented by the D<sub>1</sub> and D<sub>2</sub> bands, respectively. Amorphous carbon is associated with the D<sub>3</sub> band, while the presence of ionic impurities is related to the D<sub>4</sub> band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0043_R_d38791517e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Carbon">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the proportion of highly disordered carbons in the hard carbon material can be evaluated from the area ratio of D<sub>1</sub> to G (A<sub>D1</sub>/A<sub>G</sub>). The A<sub>D1</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> values for HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 are 2.40, 2.19, 2.11, 2.05, and 1.94, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202405921-tbl-0001">1</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3d</a>). The reduction in A<sub>D1</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> values further confirms the transformation of highly disordered carbons into pseudo-graphite carbon and graphitic-like carbons by Mo<sub>2</sub>C modification. The interlayer spacing and A<sub>D1</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> values decrease while La and Lc values increase. All the results above corroborate that Mo<sub>2</sub>C promotes the formation of pseudo-graphitic and graphitic-like carbon and effectively reduces defects, thus improving ICE and the reversible storage of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0044_R_d38791517e1411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Mater. Today">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3e</a> presents the nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms of the samples, all displaying typical Type I isotherms, indicating the existence of both micropores and mesopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0045_R_d38791517e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eng. J.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mo<sub>2</sub>C modification affects the pore structure of hard carbon (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), manifested in the significantly reduced specific surface area of Mo-modified hard carbon materials compared to HC. The specific surface area value for Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 is 6.94 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, while HC has a specific surface area of 24.07 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. The reduction is likely attributed to the introduction of molybdenum carbide, which fills some of the pore space in graphite, especially micropores and mesopores, resulting in a decrease in measurable specific surface area. A smaller surface area is advantageous for inhibiting irreversible reactions between the electrode and electrolyte to increase ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0046_R_d38791517e1443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev. B">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pore size distribution curves estimated using the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). HC exhibits a larger specific surface area and a significant amount of mesopores. These mesopores range from 2 to 50 nm and account for 85.36% of the total pore volume, which is detrimental to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. In contrast, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 shows reduced pore volume and specific surface area, favoring an enhanced ICE and cycling stability. The molybdenum content in the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample is determined to be 0.80 wt.% using inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) analysis (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) experiment is performed to portray the characteristics of closed pores in samples more effectively. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the intensity decreases as <i>Q<sup>−4</sup></i> for the lowest <i>Q</i> values, which is connected to the macroscopic surface area of the particles in hard carbon materials. When evaluating <i>Q</i> values ranging from 1 to 10 nm<sup>−1</sup>, the intensity profile displays a plateau followed by a hump, suggesting the existence of micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0044_R_d38791517e1481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Mater. Today">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SAXS patterns show large humps when the scattering vector <i>Q</i> is between 2 and 3 nm<sup>−1</sup>. These humps are ascribed to closed pores within carbon spheres, which contain voids at both the micrometer and nanometer scales locating between sp<sup>2</sup> graphite layers. Depending on SAXS patterns of samples, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibits a less pronounced peak around 2 nm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a reduction in the closed pore radius and a lower content of closed pores, which is attributed to the increased orderliness in the hard carbon structure. Meanwhile, in terms of surface openings, the introduction of molybdenum carbide fills the open pores, resulting in a decrease in the measurable specific surface area (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3e</a>). Both tests illustrate the special pore structure of molybdenum-modified hard carbon with reduced surface openings and decreased defect density, which greatly inhibits the generation of irreversible reactions and contributes to the enhanced ICE and cycling stability.</p>
            
            <p>Upon further investigation by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), the chemical components and valence states of the surface are examined. The XPS analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3g</a>) unequivocally demonstrates that the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample predominantly consists of carbon, oxygen and molybdenum elements. The high-resolution C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3h</a>) reveals five peaks, which indicate the existence of C─C, C─Mo, C─O, C═O, and O─C═O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0047_R_d38791517e1511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the high-resolution O 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information) show three peaks, which are associated with Mo─O, C═O, and C─O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0048_R_d38791517e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Power Sources">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Mo 3d orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0003">3i</a>), it can be observed that the Mo element primarily exists in three oxidation states. Peaks at 231.4 and 228.1&nbsp;eV are in connection with the 5/2 and 3/2 orbitals of divalent molybdenum (Mo<sup>2+</sup>), confirming the formation of Mo<sub>2</sub>C in the sample. In addition, peaks at 233.0 and 229.9&nbsp;eV correlate to tetravalent molybdenum (Mo<sup>4+</sup>) in molybdenum dioxide (MoO<sub>2</sub>), while peaks at 235.2 and 232.0&nbsp;eV correlate to hexavalent molybdenum (Mo<sup>6+</sup>) in molybdenum trioxide (MoO<sub>3</sub>). The presence of higher oxidation states of molybdenum is due to the occurrence of an oxide layer on the surface of molybdenum carbide exposed to air, which is also commonly reported in previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0049_R_d38791517e1541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Chem. A">49</a>-<a href="#smll202405921-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0051_R_d38791517e1541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ChemSusChem">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the C 1s spectrum of the HC samples shows no C─Mo bond peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information), and the O 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information) shows no Mo─O bond peaks. Meanwhile, the Mo 3d spectrum of the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample shows the presence of C─Mo bond, further demonstrating the presence of Mo<sub>2</sub>C. The C─Mo bond can introduce active sites on the material surface, enhancing material conductivity, structural stability, sodium storage capacity, and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0052_R_d38791517e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">52</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0053_R_d38791517e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nano-Micro Lett.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202405921-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202405921-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 is studied using cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) techniques to understand their sodium storage capabilities in a half-cell. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4a</a> displays the initial three cycles of the CV curve for Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and the potential ranges from 0.01 to 3&nbsp;V. A couple of sharp oxidation/reduction peaks can be clearly visualized ≈0.01–0.1&nbsp;V due to the insertion/extraction of sodium ions. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), both HC and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibit irreversible reduction peaks in the first CV curves, which are attributed to the generation of SEI and reactions between the electrolyte and surface functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0054_R_d38791517e1595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The visible evidence clearly indicates that the irreversible peak of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 is smaller than that of HC, which suggests a reduced irreversible sodium capacity. This reduction can be attributed to the smaller specific surface area of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, which inhibits the generation of irreversible reactions and reduces the storage capacity of irreversible sodium. In addition, the CV curves of the two subsequent cycles show high overlap, indicating that the irreversible reactions mainly occur in the first cycle. Moreover, the CV curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 overlap better than those of HC, which suggests that the sodium storage is highly reversible in the modified sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0055_R_d38791517e1606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202405921-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/196ccb62-a957-4062-b580-f99e450d6928/smll202405921-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/196ccb62-a957-4062-b580-f99e450d6928/smll202405921-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5e4d4d9d-4579-42c5-9fa3-5f4b134322b5/smll202405921-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/196ccb62-a957-4062-b580-f99e450d6928/smll202405921-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202405921-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202405921" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of molybdenum-modified hard carbon materials. a) CV curves of the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD profiles at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Comparison between sloping capacity and plateau capacity of samples. d) Cycling performance at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) GCD profiles of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 at different current densities. f) Rate performance. g) Comparison of rate performance. h) Long term cycles of samples at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 800 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The GCD curves at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4b</a>. The GCD curves for all the samples demonstrate a distinctive behavior associated with the storage of Na ions in hard carbons, which can be categorized into two main regions: the sloping zone and the plateau zone. Interestingly, compared to HC (267.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5 (321.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5 (323.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 (321.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 shows a significantly higher initial discharge capacity, reaching 410.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge and charge specific capacities of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode material in the first cycle are 410.7 and 343&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with an ICE of 83.9%. The above-mentioned data is significantly higher than the ICE of HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10, which are 72.6%, 62.6%, 76.8%, and 75.1%, respectively. The great improvement in ICE is mainly attributed to the reduction of defect density after modification, which effectively reduces the irreversible capacity loss. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the first-cycle plateau discharge capacity of all samples at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The high plateau capacity (211.44&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 is attributed to the high content of pseudo-graphite carbon. Sodium ions insert between the pseudo-graphite carbon layers and show plateau behavior below 0.1&nbsp;V, resulting in increased plateau capacity.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4d</a> shows the cyclic performance and corresponding coulombic efficiency (CE) of all samples at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibits excellent cyclic performance, with a discharge capacity of 340.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is slightly lower than the second cycle capacity of 348.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> but greatly higher than that of HC electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The capacity loss in the first cycle may result from the capture of Na<sup>+</sup> by Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 and the generation of SEI. The CE of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode remains high in the subsequent cycles, indicating reversible insertion/extraction of sodium ions and the formation of stable SEI. The increase in capacity during subsequent cycles may be attributed to the progressive opening of sodium storage sites within the electrode. The high sodium storage capacity and excellent stability of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode are owing to the cooperative interaction between pseudo-graphitic carbons and Mo<sub>2</sub>C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4e</a> shows the GCD curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode at different current densities. It can be noticed that the plateau capacity diminishes with increasing current density (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting a change in the storage mechanism of sodium ions from insertion/extraction to adsorption. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4f</a> displays the rate performance of all electrodes from 0.03 to 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibits high reversible capacities of 245.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 202.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, compared to 141.1 and 121.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for HC, respectively. The rate capability of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode is significantly superior to that of HC electrode. When the current density is switched from 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> back to 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode can recover high specific capacity again, further indicating its excellent rate capability. Compared with previous reports, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 exhibits higher reversible capacity and rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0011_R_d38791517e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0022_R_d38791517e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Carbon Energy">22</a>, <a href="#smll202405921-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0056_R_d38791517e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Energy">56</a>-<a href="#smll202405921-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0060_R_d38791517e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> is explored, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0004">4h</a> (the first three cycles represent the activation process at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 maintains a specific capacity of 214.9&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles, with a capacity retention of 94.8% (C<sub>800</sub>/C<sub>5</sub>), where C<sub>800</sub> and C<sub>5</sub> denote the specific capacity at the 800th and fifth cycles, respectively. In contrast, HC exhibits the poorest stability, with a capacity retention of 82.3% (C<sub>800</sub> /C<sub>5</sub>). Meanwhile, compared with other comparative samples, the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 anode exhibits the highest capacity and superior cycling stability at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity of 174&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles and a capacity retention of 94.28% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The above results further validate that molybdenum carbide modification can improve the structural stability and reaction kinetics in the bulk phase, resulting in excellent cycling stability for the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode. Furthermore, full-cell measurements are conducted using HC and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 as the anodes and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Clear charge–discharge plateaus are noted at 3.41 and 3.25&nbsp;V in the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, respectively. The full cell provides a high capacity of 292.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (C<sub>2</sub>), indicating the potential of Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical impedance spectrometry (EIS) conducts a deeper exploration of the kinetics of sodium storage, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0005">5a</a>. Analyzing the Nyquist plot, the crossing point with the x-axis in the high-frequency range represents the ohmic resistance (R<sub>S</sub>). Additionally, the size of the semicircle indicates the charge transfer resistance (R<sub>CT</sub>). The equivalent circuit is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). The R<sub>CT</sub> values for HC, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-2.5, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0, Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-7.5, and Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-10 are relatively small, ascribed to the ether-based electrolyte used. The unique mechanism of solvated sodium and sodium co-embedding in the ether-based electrolyte avoids the slow desolvation process and thus enhances the sodium storage kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0060_R_d38791517e1865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, the introduction of molybdenum carbide fills the surface openings, reduces surface defects and specific surface area, and creates a special surface structure, which improves the electrochemical performances, especially the cycling stability, of the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 sample. According to the EIS fitting results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), it can be observed that the R<sub>CT</sub> of the Mo<sub>2</sub>C/HC-5.0 electrode is comparatively lower than that of the remaining electrodes, providing additional evidence of its superior electrochemical kinetics. The Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i>) can be obtained by fitting the slope of <i>Z'</i> to <i>w<sup>−1/2</sup></i> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202405921-fig-0005">5b</a>. The diffusion coefficient of sodium ions (D) can be calculated by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202405921-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202405921-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202405921-bib-0015_R_d38791517e1895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202405921-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a10e4056-2dea-4987-90e4-e1186ddcebb3/smll202405921-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202405921:smll202405921-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202405921-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>

                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>

                                       <mn>2</mn>

                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>2</mn>

                                       <mi>A</mi>
